<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNdissent1.5" href="#FRdissent1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In <em>Colegrove</em>, for example, the Illinois Legislature had drawn the State's district lines under the 1901 State Apportionment Act and had not reapportioned in the four ensuing decades, "despite census figures indicating great changes in the distribution of the population."  328 U.&nbsp;S., at 569 (Black, J., dissenting).  The populations of Illinois' districts in 1945 consequently ranged from 112,000 in the least populous district to 900,000 in the most.  <em>Ibid</em>.  Nonetheless, the Court, per Justice Frankfurter, concluded that "due regard for the effective working of our Government revealed this issue to be of a peculiarly political nature and therefore not meet for judicial determination."  <em>Id</em>., at 552.  Fewer than 20 years later, the Court, confronted with a strikingly similar set of facts--a Tennessee apportionment plan set by a 1901 statute that had remained virtually unchanged despite dramatic population growth--held, in obvious tension with <em>Colegrove</em>, that the complaint stated a justiciable cause of action.  <em>Baker,</em> 369 U.&nbsp;S., at 192, 197-198.  The Court distinguished <em>Colegrove</em> as simply "a refusal to exercise equity's powers."  369 U.&nbsp;S., at 235. </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNdissent1.6" href="#FRdissent1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;"&nbsp;'[T]he potential for voter disillusion and nonparticipation is great,' as voters are forced to focus their political activities in artificial electoral units.  Intelligent voters, regardless of party affiliation, resent this sort of political manipulation of the electorate for no public purpose."  478 U.&nbsp;S., at 177 (citation omitted).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNdissent1.7" href="#FRdissent1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The reasoning in these decisions followed not only from <em>Gomillion</em> v. <em>Lightfoot,</em> 364 U.&nbsp;S. 339 (1960), see <em>Shaw I</em>, 478 U.&nbsp;S. 109, 173, n.&nbsp;12 (1986), that "[i]n some cases, proof of grotesque district shapes may, without more, provide convincing proof of unconstitutional gerrymandering."    </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNdissent1.8" href="#FRdissent1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In <em>Miller</em> v. <em>Johnson,</em> 515 U.&nbsp;S. 900, 917-919 (1995), the Court reviewed the procedures followed by the Georgia Legislature in responding to the Justice Department's objections to its original plan, and the part that the operator of its "reapportionment computer" played in designing the districts, to support its conclusion "that the legislature subordinated traditional districting principles to race."   See also <em>Bush</em> v. <em>Vera,</em> 517 U.&nbsp;S. 952, 961-962 (1996) (plurality opinion) (discussing use of computer program to manipulate district lines).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNdissent1.9" href="#FRdissent1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp; In <em>Shaw II</em>, 517 U.&nbsp;S. 899, 910 (1996), for instance, the Court considered the fact that certain reports regarding the effects of past discrimination were not before the legislature and therefore could not have played a role in the districting process. </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNdissent1.10" href="#FRdissent1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Hunt</em> v. <em>Cromartie, </em>526 U.&nbsp;S. 541, 554 (1999); <em>Bush</em> v. <em>Vera,</em> 517 U.&nbsp;S., at 986 (plurality opinion); <em>Miller</em>, 515 U.&nbsp;S., at 928; <em>Shaw I</em>, 509 U.&nbsp;S., at 659.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNdissent1.11" href="#FRdissent1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The plurality opinion seems to assume that the dissenting opinions in <em>Umbehr</em>, 518 U.&nbsp;S., at 686 (<em>Scalia</em>, J.), and <em>Rutan</em>, 497 U.&nbsp;S., at 92 (<em>Scalia</em>, J.), correctly state the law--namely, that "when a practice not expressly prohibited by the text of the Bill of Rights bears the endorsement of a long tradition of open, widespread, and unchallenged use that dates back to the beginning of the Republic, we have no proper basis for striking it down," <em>id</em>., at 95.  Cf. <em>ante</em>, at 4 (tracing the history of political gerrymanders to the beginning of the 18th century).  But "[o]ur inquiry does not begin with the judgment of history"; "[r]ather, inquiry must commence with identification of the constitutional limitations implicated by a challenged governmental practice."  <em>Elrod,</em> 427 U.&nbsp;S., at 354-355.  </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 12" name="FNdissent1.12" href="#FRdissent1.12">12</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;It goes without saying that a claim that otherwise would trigger strict scrutiny might nonetheless be nonjusticiable.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Allen</em> v. <em>Wright,</em> 468 U.&nbsp;S. 737 (1984); <em>DeFunis</em> v. <em>Odegaard,</em> 416 U.&nbsp;S. 312 (1974) <em>(per curiam).</em>  </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 13" name="FNdissent1.13" href="#FRdissent1.13">13</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The plurality explains that it is willing to "accep[t] a modest degree of unmanageability" where the "constitutional command ... is clear," but not where the "constitutional obligation ... is both dubious and severely unmanageable."  <em>Ante</em>, at 16-17.  Not only does this statement cast doubt on the plurality's faith in our racial gerrymandering cases, but its reasoning is clearly tautological.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 14" name="FNdissent1.14" href="#FRdissent1.14">14</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The plurality's reluctance to recognize the justiciability of partisan gerrymanders seems driven in part by a fear that recognizing such claims will give rise to a flood of litigation.  See <em>ante</em>, at 16.  But the list of cases that it cites in its lengthy footnote 6, <em>ante</em>, at 10-11, suggests that in the two decades since <em>Bandemer</em>, there has been an average of just three or four partisan gerrymandering cases filed every year.  That volume is obviously trivial when compared, for example, to the amount of litigation that followed our adoption of the "one-person, one-vote" rule.  See <em>Reynolds</em> v. <em>Sims,</em> 377 U.&nbsp;S. 533 (1964).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 15" name="FNdissent1.15" href="#FRdissent1.15">15</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Writing for the Court in <em>Bandemer</em>, Justice White put it well: "That the characteristics of the complaining group are not immutable or that the group has not been subject to the same historical stigma may be relevant to the manner in which the case is adjudicated, but these differences do not justify a refusal to entertain such a case."  478 U.&nbsp;S., at 125. </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 16" name="FNdissent1.16" href="#FRdissent1.16">16</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The cases that the plurality cites today, <em>ante</em>, at 10-12, n.&nbsp;6, support the conclusion that it would have been wise to endorse the views expressed in Justice Powell's dissent in <em>Bandemer</em>, 462 U.&nbsp;S. 725, 744 (1983).  I remain convinced that our opinions correctly interpreted the law.  If that standard were applied to the statewide challenge in this case, a trial of the entire case would be required.  For the purpose of deciding this case, even though I dissented from our decision in <em>Shaw I </em>and remain convinced that it was incorrectly decided, I would give the <em>Shaw</em> cases <em>stare decisis</em> effect in the political gerrymandering context.  Given the Court's illogical disposition of this case, however, in future cases I would feel free to reexamine the standing issue.  I surely would not suggest that a plaintiff would never have standing to litigate a statewide claim.  </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 17" name="FNdissent1.17" href="#FRdissent1.17">17</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;App. to Juris. Statement 138a.   </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 18" name="FNdissent1.18" href="#FRdissent1.18">18</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;As the Court explained in <em>Hays</em>, "[v]oters in [gerrymandered] districts may suffer the special representational harms [that constitutionally suspect] classifications can cause in the voting context.  On the other hand, where a plaintiff does not live in such a district, he or she does not suffer those special harms ...&nbsp;."  515 U.&nbsp;S., at 745.  </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 19" name="FNdissent1.19" href="#FRdissent1.19">19</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The plurality in <em>Bandemer,</em> 478 U.&nbsp;S., at 127, itself acknowledged that "the focus of the equal protection inquiry" in a statewide challenge "is necessarily somewhat different from that involved in the review of individual districts."  </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 20" name="FNdissent1.20" href="#FRdissent1.20">20</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Cf. <em>McConnell</em> v. <em>Federal Election Comm'n</em>, 540 U.&nbsp;S. __, __ (2003) (slip op., at 43-44) ("Just as troubling to a functioning democracy as classic <em>quid pro quo</em> corruption is the danger that officeholders will decide issues not on the merits or the desires of their constituencies, but according to the wishes of those who have made large financial contributions valued by the officeholder").  </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 21" name="FNdissent1.21" href="#FRdissent1.21">21</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Plaintiff-appellants Richard and Norma Jean Vieth are registered Democrats who reside in District 16.  App. to Juris. Statement 129a.  The complaint does not claim that they resided in a different district under the old districting scheme, nor does it anywhere allege, as it does on Furey's behalf, that District 16 in particular is irregularly shaped.  A glance at the appended map, <em>infra</em>, at 27, reveals that District 16 is not especially unusual in its contours.  Without more specific allegations regarding District 16, I would limit the analysis to District 6.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 22" name="FNdissent1.22" href="#FRdissent1.22">22</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;When her residence was located in District 13, Furey was represented by a Democrat.  App. 261.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 23" name="FNdissent1.23" href="#FRdissent1.23">23</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;App. to Juris. Statement 142a.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 24" name="FNdissent1.24" href="#FRdissent1.24">24</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;"[A]mple evidence demonstrates that many of today's congressional representatives owe their allegiance not to 'the People of the several states' but to the mercy of state legislatures."  Note, 117 Harv. L.&nbsp;Rev. 1196, 1202 (2004) (footnote omitted).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 25" name="FNdissent1.25" href="#FRdissent1.25">25</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In this sense the partisan gerrymander is the American cousin of the English "rotten borough."  In the English system, Members of Parliament were elected from geographic units that remained unchanged despite population changes wrought by the Industrial Revolution.  "Because representation was not based on population, vast inequities developed over time in the form of the so-called rotten boroughs.  Old Sarum, for instance, had no human residents--only a few sheep--yet sent the same number of representatives to Parliament as Yorkshire, with nearly a million inhabitants."  R. Zagarri, The Politics of Size: Representation in the United States, 1776-1850, p. 37 (1987) (footnote omitted).  As a result of this system, "many insignificant places returned members, while many important towns did not," and "even in large towns the members were often elected by a tiny fraction of the population."  J. Butler, The Passing of the Great Reform Bill 176 (1914).  Meanwhile, "[t]he Government bribed the patron or member or both by means of distinctions and offices or by actual cash," and "[t]he patron and member bribed the electors in the same way."  <em>Ibid</em>.  The rotten boroughs clearly would violate our familiar one-person, one-vote rule, but they were also troubling because the representative of such a borough owed his primary loyalty to his patron and the government rather than to his constituents (if he had any).  Similarly, in gerrymandered districts, instead of local groups defined by neutral criteria selecting their representatives, it is the architects of the districts who select the constituencies and, in effect, the representatives.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 26" name="FNdissent1.26" href="#FRdissent1.26">26</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In the realm of federal elections, the requirement of governmental neutrality is buttressed by this Court's recognition that the Elections Clause is not "&nbsp;'a source of power to dictate electoral outcomes, to favor or disfavor a class of candidates, or to evade important constitutional restraints.'&nbsp;"  <em>Cook</em> v. <em>Gralike, </em>531 U.&nbsp;S. 510, 523 (2001) (citing <em>U. S. Term Limits, Inc.</em> v. <em>Thornton,</em> 514 U.&nbsp;S. 779, 833-834 (1995)).  And this duty to govern impartially extends to executive and legislative officials alike.  Beginning as early as its first session in 1789, Congress has passed a number of statutes designed to guarantee that Executive Branch employees neutrally carry out their duties.  See <em>Ex parte Curtis,</em> 106 U.&nbsp;S. 371, 372-373 (1882).  Some of those laws avoided the danger that "the government itself may be made to furnish indirectly the money to defray the expenses of keeping the political party in power that happens to have for the time being the control of the public patronage."  <em>Id</em>., at 375.  It is "fundamental" that federal employees "are expected to enforce the law and execute programs of the Government without bias or favoritism for or against any political party or group or the members thereof."  <em>Civil Service Comm'n</em> v. <em>Letter Carriers,</em> 413 U.&nbsp;S. 548, 564-565 (1973).  That expectation reflects the principle that "the impartial execution of the laws" is a "great end of Government."  <em>Id</em>., at 565.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 27" name="FNdissent1.27" href="#FRdissent1.27">27</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In <em>Washington</em> v. <em>Davis,</em> we referred to an earlier challenge to a New York reapportionment statute that had failed because the plaintiffs had not shown that the statute was "&nbsp;'the product of a state contrivance to segregate on the basis of race or place of origin.'&nbsp;"  376 U.&nbsp;S. 52, 58 (1964)).  We emphasized that the Court in <em>Wright</em> had been unanimous in identifying the issue as "whether the 'boundaries ... were purposefully drawn on racial lines.'&nbsp;"  426 U.&nbsp;S., at 240 (quoting <em>Wright,</em> 376 U.&nbsp;S., at 67).  </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 28" name="FNdissent1.28" href="#FRdissent1.28">28</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;I have elsewhere explained my view that race as a factor in the districting process is no different from any other political consideration.  Creating a majority-minority district is no better and no worse than creating an Irish-American, or Polish-American, or Italian-American district.  In all events the relevant question is whether the sovereign abrogated its obligation to govern neutrally.  See <em>Karcher,</em> 462 U.&nbsp;S., at 753-754 (<em>Stevens</em>, J., concurring); <em>Mobile,</em> 446 U.&nbsp;S., at 88 (<em>Stevens</em>, J., concurring in judgment); <em>Cousins</em> v. <em>City Council of Chicago</em>, 466 F.&nbsp;2d 830, 850-853 (CA7 1972) (Stevens, J., dissenting).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 29" name="FNdissent1.29" href="#FRdissent1.29">29</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The plurality's long discussion of the history of political gerrymanders is interesting, <em>ante</em>, at 4-7, but it surely is not intended to suggest that the vintage of an invidious practice--even "an American political tradition as old as the Republic," <em>Board of Comm'rs, Wabaunsee Cty.</em> v. <em>Umbehr,</em> 518 U.&nbsp;S. 668, 688 (1996) (<em>Scalia</em>, J., dissenting)--should insulate it from constitutional review.  Compare, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Bradwell</em> v. <em>State,</em> 16 Wall. 130 (1873), with <em>Nevada Dept. of Human Resources </em>v. <em>Hibbs, </em>538 U.&nbsp;S. 721, 729 (2003).  The historical discussion might be relevant if it attempted to justify political gerrymandering as an acceptable use of governmental power.  In the end, however, the plurality's defense of its position comes down to the unconvincing assertion that it lacks the juridical capacity to administer the standards the Court fashioned in its recent racial gerrymandering jurisprudence.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 30" name="FNdissent1.30" href="#FRdissent1.30">30</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Bandemer,</em> 478 U.&nbsp;S. 109 (plurality opinion); <em>Gaffney</em> v. <em>Cummings,</em> 412 U.&nbsp;S. 735, 754 (1973); <em>Whitcomb</em> v. <em>Chavis,</em> 403 U.&nbsp;S. 124, 143 (1971); <em>Burns</em> v. <em>Richardson,</em> 384 U.&nbsp;S. 73, 88 (1966); <em>Fortson</em> v. <em>Dorsey,</em> 379 U.&nbsp;S. 433, 439 (1965).  Consistent with these statements, the District Court in a recent case correctly described political gerrymandering as "a purely partisan exercise" and "an abuse of power that, at its core, evinces a fundamental distrust of voters, serving the self-interest of the political parties at the expense of the public good."  App. to Juris. Statement in <em>Balderas </em>v. <em>Texas, </em>O. T. 2001, No. 01-1196, p. 10.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 31" name="FNdissent1.31" href="#FRdissent1.31">31</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See <em>Shaw</em> <em>II,</em> 517 U.&nbsp;S., at 918 (<em>Stevens</em>, J., dissenting); <em>Bush</em> v. <em>Vera,</em> 517 U.&nbsp;S., at 1033-1034 (<em>Stevens</em>, J., dissenting); <em>Miller,</em> 515 U.&nbsp;S., at 947-948 (<em>Ginsburg</em>, J., dissenting).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 32" name="FNdissent1.32" href="#FRdissent1.32">32</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Because race so seldom provides a rational basis for a governmental decision, racial classifications almost always fail to survive "rational basis" scrutiny.  But "[n]ot every decision influenced by race is equally objectionable."  <em>Grutter</em> v. <em>Bollinger, </em>539 U.&nbsp;S. 306, 327 (2003).  When race is used as the basis for making predictive political judgments, it may be as reliable (or unreliable) as other group characteristics, such as political affiliation, economic status, or national origin.  The fact that race is an immutable characteristic does not mean that there is anything immutable or certain about the political behavior of the members of any racial class.  See <em>Mobile</em> v. <em>Bolden</em>, 446 U.&nbsp;S. 55, 88 (1980) (<em>Stevens</em>, J., concurring in judgment).  Registered Republicans of all races sometimes vote for Democratic candidates, and vice versa.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The plurality asserts that a person's politics, unlike her race, is not readily "discernible."  <em>Ante</em>, at 17.  But that assertion is belied by the evidence that the architects of political gerrymanders seem to have no difficulty in discerning the voters' political affiliation.  After all, eligibility to vote in primary elections often requires the citizen to register her party affiliation, but it never requires her to register her race.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 33" name="FNdissent1.33" href="#FRdissent1.33">33</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The one-person, one-vote rule obviously constitutes a neutral districting criterion, but our gerrymandering cases have never cited that principle as one of the traditional criteria "that may serve to defeat a claim that a district has been gerrymandered on racial lines."  <em>Shaw I</em>, 509 U.&nbsp;S., at 647.  Thus, I would require that a district be justified with reference to both the one-person, one-vote rule and some other neutral criterion.  See <em>Bandemer</em>, 478 U.&nbsp;S., at 162, 168 (Powell, J., concurring in part and dissenting in part).  </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 34" name="FNdissent1.34" href="#FRdissent1.34">34</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The California districting scheme at issue in <em>Badham</em> featured a large number of districts with highly irregular shapes, all designed, the plaintiff-appellants alleged, to dilute Republican voting strength throughout the State.  See Juris. Statement in <em>Badham</em> v. <em>Eu</em>, O. T. 1987, No. 87-1818, Exh. D, p. 77a.  Three Members of this Court dissented from the summary affirmance in <em>Badham</em> and would have noted probable jurisdiction.  488 U.&nbsp;S. 1024 (1989).  </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 35" name="FNdissent1.35" href="#FRdissent1.35">35</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;App. to Juris. Statement 135a.  </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 36" name="FNdissent1.36" href="#FRdissent1.36">36</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Id</em>., at 136a.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 37" name="FNdissent1.37" href="#FRdissent1.37">37</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Id</em>., at 142a.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 38" name="FNdissent1.38" href="#FRdissent1.38">38</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Id</em>., at 143a.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 39" name="FNdissent1.39" href="#FRdissent1.39">39</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Id</em>., at 140a.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 40" name="FNdissent1.40" href="#FRdissent1.40">40</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Id</em>., at 143a.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 41" name="FNdissent1.41" href="#FRdissent1.41">41</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Ibid</em>.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 42" name="FNdissent1.42" href="#FRdissent1.42">42</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Id</em>., at 135a. </p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3><br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNdissent2.1" href="#FRdissent2.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;And the plurality says the dissenters labor still in vain today, <em>ante</em>, at 22-23; I join in <em>Justice Breyer</em>'s response, <em>post</em>, at 14-15.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNdissent2.2" href="#FRdissent2.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The plurality says it would not be easy to define such a group, because "a person's politics is rarely as readily discernible--and <em>never</em> as permanently discernible--as a person's race," <em>ante</em>, at 17.  But anytime political gerrymandering has been shown to occur, evidence must at least imply that the defendants themselves sat down, identified the relevant groups, and set out to concentrate the vote of one and dilute that of the others.  If a plaintiff has the evidence, a court can figure out what was going on.  In major-party cases I do not see any problem with permitting a plaintiff to allege that he is a registered Republican, for example, and that the state legislature set out through gerrymandering to minimize the number of Republicans elected.  If references to registration will not serve, a plaintiff will need to show the criteria for partisan affiliation employed by the defendants in the challenged districting process.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNdissent2.3" href="#FRdissent2.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Those measures, as defined by Professors Pildes and Niemi, include <em>dispersion</em>, the ratio of the area of the district to the area of the smallest circle that circumscribes the district, Pildes &amp; Niemi, at 554-555; <em>perimeter</em>, the ratio of the area of the district to the area of the circle whose diameter equals the length of the area's perimeter, <em>id.</em>, at 555-556; and <em>population</em>, the ratio of the district's population to the population contained by the minimum convex figure that encloses the district (or "rubber-band" area), <em>id.</em>, at 556-557, and n.&nbsp;206.  The population measure can also be taken using the district's circumscribing circle in the denominator.  <em>Id.</em>, at 557.  See also Polsby &amp; Popper, The Third Criterion: Compactness as a Procedural Safeguard Against Partisan Gerrymandering, 9 Yale L. &amp; Pol'y Rev. 301, 339-351 (1991) (discussing quantitative measures of compactness, and favoring the perimeter measure as superior for antigerrymandering purposes); Schwartzberg, Reapportionment, Gerrymanders, and the Notion of "Compactness," 50 Minn L. Rev. 443 (1966) (discussing proposed legislation that would have applied a variant of the perimeter measure).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNdissent2.4" href="#FRdissent2.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;It would not necessarily involve redrawing other noncontiguous districts, and I would not permit a plaintiff to ask for such a remedy unless he first made out a prima facie case as to multiple districts.  See <em>infra</em>, at 11.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNdissent2.5" href="#FRdissent2.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Amici</em> JoAnn Erfer et&nbsp;al. suggest that a political party strong enough to redistrict without the other's approval is analogous to a firm that exercises monopolistic control over a market, and that the ability to exercise such unilateral control should therefore trigger "heightened constitutional scrutiny."  Brief 18-19 (citing <em>Terry</em> v. <em>Adams,</em> 345 U.&nbsp;S. 461 (1953), the Texas Jaybird primary case).  See also Issacharoff, Gerrymandering and Political Cartels, 116 Harv. L.&nbsp;Rev. 593 (2002); Issacharoff &amp; Pildes, Politics as Markets: Partisan Lockups of the Democratic Process, 50 Stan. L.&nbsp;Rev. 643 (1998).  The analogy to antitrust is an intriguing one that may prove fruitful, though I do not embrace it at this point out of caution about a wholesale conceptual transfer from economics to politics.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNdissent2.6" href="#FRdissent2.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Some commentators have criticized <em>Gaffney</em> itself for failing to account for the harm of bipartisan political gerrymandering to the political process.  <em>E.g.,</em> Issacharoff, Political Cartels, <em>supra</em>, at 613 ("<em>Gaffney</em> illustrates the problem of the use of a discrimination model unmoored to any positive account of the electoral process").  <em>Gaffney </em>is settled law, and for today's purposes I would take as given its approval of bipartisan gerrymanders, with their associated goal of incumbent protection.  The plurality may be correct, <em>ante</em>, at 28-29, that the test I propose could catch more objectionable gerrymanders if we rejected incumbent protection as an acceptable purpose of districting.  But I am wary of lumping all measures aimed at incumbent protection together at this point, and I think we would gain a better sense of what to do if we waited upon the experience of the district courts in assessing particular efforts at incumbency protection offered by the States in responding to prima facie cases.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNdissent2.7" href="#FRdissent2.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;It is worth a moment to address the plurality's charge that any judicial remedy for political gerrymandering necessarily assumes a right to proportional representation.  <em>Ante</em>, at 18 ("Deny it as appellants may (and do), [their] standard rests upon the principle that groups (or at least political-action groups) have a right to proportional representation").  I agree with this Court's earlier statements that the Constitution guarantees no right to proportional representation.  See <em>Davis</em> v. <em>Bandemer, </em>478 U.&nbsp;S. 109, 130 (1986) (plurality opinion) (citing <em>Whitcomb</em> v. <em>Chavis,</em> 403 U.&nbsp;S. 124 (1971), and <em>White</em> v. <em>Regester,</em> 412 U.&nbsp;S. 755 (1973)).  It does not follow that the Constitution permits every state action intended to achieve any extreme form of disproportionate representation.  "Proportional representation" usually refers to a set of procedural mechanisms used to guarantee, with more or less precision, that a political party's seats in the legislature will be proportionate to its share of the vote.  See generally S. Issacharoff, P. Karlan, &amp; R. Pildes, The Law of Democracy, 1089-1172 (rev. 2d ed. 2002) (discussing voting systems other than the single-member district).  The Constitution requires a State to adopt neither those mechanisms nor their goal of giving a party seats proportionate to its vote.</p>
 
        <p></p>
        <p></p>
    </div>

</div>

_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory = function(nameString, href) {
  // TODO remove this once the correct PA is being used for the lid
  if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') && _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa ) {
    nameString = nameString.replace(/pa:[0-9]+/, "pa:"+_satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa);  
  }
  _satellite.logger.debug('_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory nameString: '+nameString);

  // sample name: "&lid=clk:fs/prod:dts/cid:5088127/msa:259/pa:1313/sub:3107462/pos:1"
  var s = nameString;
  s = s.substr(5);
  var lid = s;
  s = s.replaceAll(':','":"');
  s = s.replaceAll('/','","');
  s = '{"'+s+'"}'
  var adInfo = JSON.parse(s);
  adInfo.lid = lid;
  adInfo.paid = (typeof adInfo.sub != 'undefined');
  adInfo.adId = (adInfo.paid ? adInfo.sub : adInfo.cid);
  var map = {
    'ph':'phone',
    'fs':'website',
    'pv':'profile',
    'cu':'contact',
    'vr':'review-read',
    'wr':'review-write',
    'sl':'super-lawyers'
  };  
  adInfo.type = map[adInfo.clk];   
  adInfo.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id')+'-'+adInfo.adId+'-'+(new Date().getTime());
  
  if( href && adInfo.type == 'phone' ) {
    adInfo.detail = href.replace(/[^\d]/g,'');
    if( adInfo.detail.length > 10 ) {
      adInfo.detail = adInfo.detail.substr(adInfo.detail.length-10);
    }
  }  
  
  return adInfo;
}

_satellite.ads.trackAdEngagement = function(adInfo) {
  if( typeof adInfo.adId == 'undefined' ) {
    return false;
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads = {};
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements = [];
  }
  for( var i=0; i < FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.length; i++ ) {
    if( FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].type == adInfo.type && FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].adId == adInfo.adId ) {
      return true;
    }
  }

  FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.push(adInfo);
  return false;
}

function setTrackerCookie(name, value)
{
  var hostParts = location.host.split('.').reverse();
  var cookieDomain = '.' + hostParts[1] + '.' + hostParts[0];
  document.cookie = name + '=' + value + ';domain=' + cookieDomain + ';path=/';
}

window.addEventListener("load", function(event) {
  try {

    _satellite.trackSearchSource = function(type){
      let toStore = type;
      if(type.indexOf("Top-Nav")>-1 || type.indexOf("Next Steps")>-1 ){
        const pageType = _satellite.getVar("page_page_type");
        if(pageType == "article") toStore += " Article";
        if(pageType == "index") toStore += " Index";
      }
      var practiceCode = _satellite.getVar("practice_code");
      if (practiceCode) {
        toStore+= "|"+practiceCode;
      }
      _satellite.logger.info('s_dsource',toStore);
      _satellite.writeCrossSubdomainCookie("s_dsource", toStore);
    };

    _satellite.slowDown = function(e, goTo){
      e.preventDefault(); // prevent default anchor behavior
      setTimeout(function() {
        window.location = goTo;
      }, 300);
    }
    
    //CTA Search - enter & click
    const nextStepsForm = document.querySelector('.nextSteps form');
    _satellite.logger.info('nextStepsForm',nextStepsForm);
    if (nextStepsForm) {
          nextStepsForm.addEventListener('submit', function(e) {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Next Steps");
        });
    }


    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - Enter
    const searchComponents = document.querySelectorAll('#search-component,.hero-content form, .search-secondary form');
    _satellite.logger.info('searchComponents',searchComponents);
    searchComponents.forEach((component) => {
      component.addEventListener('keypress', function(e) {
        if (e.key === 'Enter') {
          if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
            _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
            _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
          } else {
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
              _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
          }
        }
      });
    })
    

    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - click
    const submitButtons = document.querySelectorAll('submit-button, .hero-content input[type="submit"], .search-secondary input[type="submit"]')
    submitButtons.forEach((button) => {
      _satellite.logger.info('button',button);
      button.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        if (location.host + location.pathname === "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
        } else {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
        }
      });
    })

    //Homepage search
    if (location.host + location.pathname === "www.findlaw.com/") {
      const forms = document.querySelectorAll("form[action*='stdSearch_process.jsp']");
      _satellite.logger.info('forms',forms);

        _satellite.logger.info('form',form);
        form.addEventListener('submit', function() {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Home");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Home');
        });
      });
    }

    //CTA
    const links = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="fli="], .articleHeading p a[href*="lawyers.findlaw.com"]');
    _satellite.logger.info('links',links);
    links.forEach((link) => {
      link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        const goTo = link.getAttribute('href');
        _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
        let source = link.dataset.searchLocation || '';
        if (!source) {
          if (link.closest('.cta-public')) {
            source = 'CTA: Banner';
          } else {
            source = 'CTA: Inline Link';
          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        }
      });
    });

    //Right Rail
    const sidebarLawyerSearch = document.querySelector('.sidebarLawyerSearch');
    _satellite.logger.info('sidebarLawyerSearch',sidebarLawyerSearch);
    if (sidebarLawyerSearch) {
          sidebarLawyerSearch.addEventListener('click', function(event) {
            if (event.target.getAttribute('href') && event.target.getAttribute('href').includes('lawyers.findlaw.com')) {
              const goTo = event.target.getAttribute('href');
              _satellite.slowDown(event, goTo);
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Link Module: Right Rail");
            }
          });
    }

    //Browse
    if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
      const lawyerLinks = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="/lawyer"]');
      _satellite.logger.info('lawyerLinks',lawyerLinks);
      lawyersLinks.forEach((link) => {
        link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
          const goTo = this.getAttribute('href');
          _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
          let source = "";
          if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statepractice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Popular Cities";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/state/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: State";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statecounty/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Top Counties";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/practice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Legal Issue";
          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        });
      });
    }
    
    //data-search-location
    const linksWithSearchLocation = document.querySelectorAll('[data-search-location] a, a[data-search-location]');
    _satellite.logger.info('linksWithSearchLocation',linksWithSearchLocation);
    linksWithSearchLocation.forEach((link) => {
      link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        var goTo = this.getAttribute('href');
        _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
        var source = e.currentTarget.dataset.searchLocation || e.currentTarget.dataset.closest("[data-search-location]").dataset.searchLocation;
        _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
        _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
      });
    });

  } catch (exp) {
    _satellite.logger.log("Issue with 'Search Source' Rule");
  }
  
});
